# The expanding repertoire of ESCRT functions in cell biology and disease

## Abstract

The endosomal sorting complex required for transport (ESCRT) is a multicomplex machinery comprising proteins that are conserved from bacteria to humans and has diverse roles in regulating the dynamics of cellular membranes. ESCRT functions have far-reaching consequences for cell biological processes such as intracellular traffic, membrane repair, cell signalling, metabolic regulation, cell division and genome maintenance. Here we review recent insights that emphasize the pathophysiological consequences of ESCRT dysfunctions, including infections, immune disorders, cancers and neurological diseases. We highlight the possibilities of using our knowledge about ESCRT structures and functions for drug discovery.

## Main

The ESCRT machinery consists of the core complexes ESCRT-I, ESCRT-II and ESCRT-III, the VPS4 ATPase complex, the recruitment factors ESCRT-0 and CHMP7, and the adaptor protein ALIX (Table [1](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Tab1)). Since the initial discovery of the ESCRT[1](#ref-CR1 "Katzmann, D. J., Babst, M. & Emr, S. D. Ubiquitin-dependent sorting into the multivesicular body pathway requires the function of a conserved endosomal protein sorting complex, ESCRT-I. Cell 106, 145–155 (2001)."),[2](#ref-CR2 "Babst, M., Katzmann, D. J., Snyder, W. B., Wendland, B. & Emr, S. D. Endosome-associated complex, ESCRT-II, recruits transport machinery for protein sorting at the multivesicular body. Dev. Cell 3, 283–289 (2002)."),[3](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR3 "Babst, M., Katzmann, D. J., Estepa-Sabal, E. J., Meerloo, T. & Emr, S. D. Escrt-III: an endosome-associated heterooligomeric protein complex required for mvb sorting. Dev. Cell 3, 271–282 (2002). References 1–3 identify ESCRT-I–III through genetic and biochemical analyses of Saccharomyces cerevisiae vacuolar protein sorting mutants.") machinery, it has become clear that the unique and indispensable function of ESCRTs in animal cells is to carry out ‘reverse-topology’ membrane scission[4](#ref-CR4 "Hurley, J. H. & Hanson, P. I. Membrane budding and scission by the ESCRT machinery: it’s all in the neck. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 11, 556–566 (2010)."),[5](#ref-CR5 "Henne, W. M., Stenmark, H. & Emr, S. D. Molecular mechanisms of the membrane sculpting ESCRT pathway. Cold Spring Harb. Perspect. Biol. 5, a016766 (2013)."),[6](#ref-CR6 "Schoneberg, J., Lee, I. H., Iwasa, J. H. & Hurley, J. H. Reverse-topology membrane scission by the ESCRT proteins. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 18, 5–17 (2017)."),[7](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR7 "McCullough, J., Frost, A. & Sundquist, W. I. Structures, functions, and dynamics of ESCRT-III/Vps4 membrane remodeling and fission complexes. Annu. Rev. Cell Dev. Biol. 34, 85–109 (2018)."). ESCRTs sever the necks of membranes that bud away from the cytosol. The invagination of the limiting membrane into the endosome lumen, followed by severing of the bud necks, is responsible for the biogenesis of multivesicular endosomes (MVEs), a key step in endolysosomal degradation. A similar process occurs when membrane-enveloped viruses, such as HIV, bud away from the plasma membrane. The severing of the intercellular bridge in the abscission step of cytokinesis is topologically equivalent, as is the closure of autophagosomes. Plasma membrane and lysosome membrane repair also occur via topologically equivalent budding events. In some instances, ESCRTs carry out ‘normal-topology’ scission[8](#ref-CR8 "Allison, R. et al. An ESCRT–spastin interaction promotes fission of recycling tubules from the endosome. J. Cell Biol. 202, 527–543 (2013)."),[9](#ref-CR9 "Mast, F. D. et al. ESCRT-III is required for scissioning new peroxisomes from the endoplasmic reticulum. J. Cell Biol. 217, 2087–2102 (2018)."),[10](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR10 "McCullough, J. et al. Structure and membrane remodeling activity of ESCRT-III helical polymers. Science 350, 1548–1551 (2015)."), and there are examples of non-ESCRT mechanisms for reverse-topology scission[11](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR11 "Trajkovic, K. et al. Ceramide triggers budding of exosome vesicles into multivesicular endosomes. Science 319, 1244–1247 (2008)."),[12](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR12 "Stuffers, S., Sem Wegner, C., Stenmark, H. & Brech, A. Multivesicular endosome biogenesis in the absence of ESCRTs. Traffic 10, 925–937 (2009)."). However, the ESCRT proteins are by far the most widespread class of reverse-topology scission machinery.

**Table 1 ESCRT proteins in yeast and humans**

[Full size table](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y/tables/1)

The scission reaction (Fig. [1](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig1)) takes place in four steps: (1) recruitment; (2) collar assembly; (3) membrane constriction and (4) recycling. In most cases of ESCRT biology, unique factors converge on the recruitment (step 1) of ESCRT-I and/or ALIX as the two most upstream components[13](#ref-CR13 "Strack, B., Calistri, A., Craig, S., Popova, E. & Gottlinger, H. G. AIP1/ALIX is a binding partner for HIV-1 p6 and EIAV p9 functioning in virus budding. Cell 114, 689–699 (2003)."),[14](#ref-CR14 "von Schwedler, U. K. et al. The protein network of HIV budding. Cell 114, 701–713 (2003)."),[15](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR15 "Christ, L. et al. ALIX and ESCRT-I/II function as parallel ESCRT-III recruiters in cytokinetic abscission. J. Cell Biol. 212, 499–513 (2016)."). Collar assembly[16](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR16 "Flower, T. G. et al. A helical assembly of human ESCRT-I scaffolds reverse-topology membrane scission. Nat. Struct. Mol. Biol. 27, 570–580 (2020).") (step 2) provides a scaffold, supporting the membrane geometry for downstream components. Membrane constriction (step 3) is performed by the ESCRT-III proteins (CHMPs (charged multivesicular body proteins)). CHMPs are small α-helical hairpin proteins that form spiral[17](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR17 "Hanson, P. I., Roth, R., Lin, Y. & Heuser, J. E. Plasma membrane deformation by circular arrays of ESCRT-III protein filaments. J. Cell Biol. 180, 389–402 (2008).") and superhelical assemblies[7](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR7 "McCullough, J., Frost, A. & Sundquist, W. I. Structures, functions, and dynamics of ESCRT-III/Vps4 membrane remodeling and fission complexes. Annu. Rev. Cell Dev. Biol. 34, 85–109 (2018)."),[10](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR10 "McCullough, J. et al. Structure and membrane remodeling activity of ESCRT-III helical polymers. Science 350, 1548–1551 (2015)."),[18](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR18 "Lata, S. et al. Helical structures of ESCRT-III are disassembled by VPS4. Science 321, 1354–1357 (2008).") that deform and constrict membranes. In their resting state, CHMP proteins in the cytosolic reservoir are autoinhibited by their C-terminal domains[19](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR19 "Shim, S., Kimpler, L. A. & Hanson, P. I. Structure/function analysis of four core ESCRT-III proteins reveals common regulatory role for extreme C-terminal domain. Traffic 8, 1068–1079 (2007)."). CHMP proteins pack tightly into functional arrays[10](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR10 "McCullough, J. et al. Structure and membrane remodeling activity of ESCRT-III helical polymers. Science 350, 1548–1551 (2015)."), and their functions are vulnerable to perturbation of the C-terminal domains[19](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR19 "Shim, S., Kimpler, L. A. & Hanson, P. I. Structure/function analysis of four core ESCRT-III proteins reveals common regulatory role for extreme C-terminal domain. Traffic 8, 1068–1079 (2007)."). CHMP polymers and membranes do not spontaneously dissociate, but work with the AAA+ ATPase VPS4A or VPS4B (VPS4A/B), which releases CHMPs from the membrane and remodels CHMP polymers in membrane scission[20](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR20 "Schoneberg, J. et al. ATP-dependent force generation and membrane scission by ESCRT-III and Vps4. Science 362, 1423–1428 (2018)."),[21](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR21 "Azad, K. et al. Structural basis of CHMP2A–CHMP3 ESCRT-III polymer assembly and membrane cleavage. Nat. Struct. Mol. Biol. 30, 81–90 (2023)."). VPS4A/B recycles CHMPs (step 4) by unfolding them and passing them through the central pore of the AAA+ ATPase hexamer[22](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR22 "Yang, B., Stjepanovic, G., Shen, Q., Martin, A. & Hurley, J. H. Vps4 disassembles an ESCRT-III filament by global unfolding and processive translocation. Nat. Struct. Mol. Biol. 22, 492–498 (2015)."),[23](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR23 "Han, H., Monroe, N., Sundquist, W. I., Shen, P. S. & Hill, C. P. The AAA ATPase Vps4 binds ESCRT-III substrates through a repeating array of dipeptide-binding pockets. eLife 6, e31324 (2017)."). Most of the pathogenic effects of ESCRT perturbations (Table [1](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Tab1)) are associated with one or more of the core steps in the scission mechanisms.

**Fig. 1: ESCRTs in reverse-topology membrane scission.**

![Fig. 1: ESCRTs in reverse-topology membrane scission.](//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-025-08950-y/MediaObjects/41586_2025_8950_Fig1_HTML.png)

[Full size image](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y/figures/1)

Top, ESCRT recruitment begins with unique mechanisms specific to each of their cellular functions. In most pathways, recruitment begins with ESCRT-I or ALIX. ESCRT-I is known to form a collar assembly at the necks of membrane buds or tubes directed away from the cytosol. ESCRT-I functions together with ESCRT-II, whereas ALIX can function with ESCRT-I or independently. Membrane constriction proceeds with the recruitment of the CHMP proteins (ESCRT-III) and their remodelling by the AAA+ ATPase VPS4A/B. After membrane constriction proceeds to scission, remaining CHMP proteins are recycled back to the cytosol by VPS4. Bottom, structures of key ESCRT proteins and their Protein Data Bank ([https://www.rcsb.org/](https://www.rcsb.org/)) IDs.

## Cellular functions of ESCRTs

The reverse-topology membrane scission executed by ESCRTs controls multiple membrane dynamics processes in cells (Fig. [2](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig2)).

**Fig. 2: Cellular functions of ESCRTs.**

![Fig. 2: Cellular functions of ESCRTs.](//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-025-08950-y/MediaObjects/41586_2025_8950_Fig2_HTML.png)

[Full size image](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y/figures/2)

ESCRTs (depicted with spirals) mediate reverse-topology membrane scission in a variety of cellular contexts, including MVEs and exosome biogenesis, micro-autophagy, autophagosome sealing, sealing of the nascent nuclear envelope, plasma membrane repair, cytokinetic abscission, nuclear envelope repair, lysosome repair, ectosome (microvesicle) biogenesis and viral budding. NPC, nuclear pore complex.

### Endosomal sorting, MVEs and exosome biogenesis

ESCRT-0 is recruited to endosome membranes through recognition of phosphatidylinositol 3-phosphate, possibly through coincidence detection of cargo molecules[24](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR24 "Raiborg, C. et al. FYVE and coiled-coil domains determine the specific localisation of Hrs to early endosomes. J. Cell Sci. 114, 2255–2263 (2001)."),[25](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR25 "Wenzel, E. M. et al. Concerted ESCRT and clathrin recruitment waves define the timing and morphology of intraluminal vesicle formation. Nat. Commun. 9, 2932 (2018)."). ESCRT-0 functions to capture ubiquitinated endosomal cargoes through multivalent interactions with ubiquitin moieties on the cargoes[26](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR26 "Bache, K. G., Raiborg, C., Mehlum, A. & Stenmark, H. STAM and Hrs are subunits of a multivalent ubiquitin-binding complex on early endosomes. J. Biol. Chem. 278, 12513–12521 (2003)."),[27](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR27 "Ren, X. & Hurley, J. H. VHS domains of ESCRT-0 cooperate in high-avidity binding to polyubiquitinated cargo. EMBO J. 29, 1045–1054 (2010)."), and cargo sorting is facilitated by concentration of ESCRT-0 in clathrin-containing microdomains of the endosome membrane[25](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR25 "Wenzel, E. M. et al. Concerted ESCRT and clathrin recruitment waves define the timing and morphology of intraluminal vesicle formation. Nat. Commun. 9, 2932 (2018)."),[28](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR28 "Raiborg, C. et al. Hrs sorts ubiquitinated proteins into clathrin-coated microdomains of early endosomes. Nat. Cell Biol. 4, 394–398 (2002)."). In most known cases, the cargoes are transmembrane proteins such as growth factor receptors or lysosomal hydrolases[29](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR29 "Katzmann, D. J., Stefan, C. J., Babst, M. & Emr, S. D. Vps27 recruits ESCRT machinery to endosomes during MVB sorting. J. Cell Biol. 162, 413–423 (2003)."),[30](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR30 "Bache, K. G., Brech, A., Mehlum, A. & Stenmark, H. Hrs regulates multivesicular body formation via ESCRT recruitment to endosomes. J. Cell Biol. 162, 435–442 (2003)."), but cytosolic cargoes may also be recognized by ESCRT-0 if they are ubiquitinated and recruited to endosomes[31](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR31 "Liu, X. M. et al. ESCRTs cooperate with a selective autophagy receptor to mediate vacuolar targeting of soluble cargos. Mol. Cell 59, 1035–1042 (2015).").

The heterotetrameric ESCRT-I[1](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR1 "Katzmann, D. J., Babst, M. & Emr, S. D. Ubiquitin-dependent sorting into the multivesicular body pathway requires the function of a conserved endosomal protein sorting complex, ESCRT-I. Cell 106, 145–155 (2001)."),[32](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR32 "Kostelansky, M. S. et al. Molecular architecture and functional model of the complete yeast ESCRT-I heterotetramer. Cell 129, 485–498 (2007)."),[33](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR33 "Wunderley, L., Brownhill, K., Stefani, F., Tabernero, L. & Woodman, P. The molecular basis for selective assembly of the UBAP1-containing endosome-specific ESCRT-I complex. J. Cell Sci. 127, 663–672 (2014).") is recruited to endosomes by ESCRT-0[29](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR29 "Katzmann, D. J., Stefan, C. J., Babst, M. & Emr, S. D. Vps27 recruits ESCRT machinery to endosomes during MVB sorting. J. Cell Biol. 162, 413–423 (2003)."),[30](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR30 "Bache, K. G., Brech, A., Mehlum, A. & Stenmark, H. Hrs regulates multivesicular body formation via ESCRT recruitment to endosomes. J. Cell Biol. 162, 435–442 (2003)."). ESCRT-I and another heterotetramer, ESCRT-II (which contains two EAP20 subunits), form a supercomplex in vitro and might be recruited simultaneously to endosome membranes[34](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR34 "Boura, E. et al. Solution structure of the ESCRT-I and -II supercomplex: implications for membrane budding and scission. Structure 20, 874–886 (2012)."). Similar to ESCRT-0, ESCRT-I and ESCRT-II contain ubiquitin-binding domains that contribute to protein sorting. Beyond cargo sorting, the ESCRT-I–ESCRT-II supercomplex has the capacity to cause membrane invaginations and is likely to initiate intralumenal vesicle (ILV) biogenesis[35](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR35 "Wollert, T. & Hurley, J. H. Molecular mechanism of multivesicular body biogenesis by ESCRT complexes. Nature 464, 864–869 (2010)."),[36](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR36 "Liese, S. et al. Protein crowding mediates membrane remodeling in upstream ESCRT-induced formation of intraluminal vesicles. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 117, 28614–28624 (2020).").

ESCRT-III consists of CHMP proteins that form oligomeric filaments and is recruited to endosomes through binding of the two EAP20 subunits of ESCRT-II to the ESCRT-III subunit CHMP6[3](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR3 "Babst, M., Katzmann, D. J., Estepa-Sabal, E. J., Meerloo, T. & Emr, S. D. Escrt-III: an endosome-associated heterooligomeric protein complex required for mvb sorting. Dev. Cell 3, 271–282 (2002). References 1–3 identify ESCRT-I–III through genetic and biochemical analyses of Saccharomyces cerevisiae vacuolar protein sorting mutants."),[37](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR37 "Teis, D., Saksena, S., Judson, B. L. & Emr, S. D. ESCRT-II coordinates the assembly of ESCRT-III filaments for cargo sorting and multivesicular body vesicle formation. EMBO J. 29, 871–883 (2010)."). This ESCRT-III subunit then functions as seed for assembly of parallel filaments containing additional ESCRT-III subunits. The ATPase VPS4 is essential for editing and disassembly of ESCRT-III, which escapes sorting into ILVs[38](#ref-CR38 "Babst, M., Sato, T. K., Banta, L. M. & Emr, S. D. Endosomal transport function in yeast requires a novel AAA-type ATPase, Vps4p. EMBO J. 16, 1820–1831 (1997)."),[39](#ref-CR39 "Stuchell-Brereton, M. D. et al. ESCRT-III recognition by VPS4 ATPases. Nature 449, 740–744 (2007)."),[40](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR40 "Adell, M. A. Y. et al. Recruitment dynamics of ESCRT-III and Vps4 to endosomes and implications for reverse membrane budding. eLife 6, e31652 (2017).").

ESCRTs are important for formation of exosomes, small extracellular vesicles that are released from cells when MVEs fuse with the plasma membrane[41](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR41 "van Niel, G., D’Angelo, G. & Raposo, G. Shedding light on the cell biology of extracellular vesicles. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 19, 213–228 (2018)."). The ESCRT components TSG101 and ALIX are often used as markers for exosomes, indicating that a fraction of ESCRT components fail to dissociate from the membrane prior to ILV scission. Exosomes have been reported to mediate intercellular signalling, but it remains unclear whether ESCRT-related diseases can be directly attributed to failure of exosome biogenesis.

### Micro-autophagy

Micro-autophagy involves degradation of cytoplasmic cargoes by their direct engulfment into vacuoles, endosomes or lysosomes[42](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR42 "Wang, L., Klionsky, D. J. & Shen, H. M. The emerging mechanisms and functions of microautophagy. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 24, 186–203 (2023)."). It is mediated by ESCRTs through mechanisms that resemble endosomal sorting and ILV formation. For some cargoes, including lysosomal enzymes and proteasomes, the entire ESCRT machinery, including ESCRT-0, is involved[43](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR43 "Li, J. & Hochstrasser, M. Selective microautophagy of proteasomes is initiated by ESCRT-0 and is promoted by proteasome ubiquitylation. J. Cell Sci. 135, jcs259393 (2022)."),[44](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR44 "Yang, X. et al. TORC1 regulates vacuole membrane composition through ubiquitin- and ESCRT-dependent microautophagy. J. Cell Biol. 219, e201902127 (2020)."), whereas other microautophagic cargoes, including autophagy receptors, are engulfed in the absence of ESCRT-0, ESCRT-I, ESCRT-II and some ESCRT-III components[45](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR45 "Mejlvang, J. et al. Starvation induces rapid degradation of selective autophagy receptors by endosomal microautophagy. J. Cell Biol. 217, 3640–3655 (2018)."). This probably reflects differences in how various cargoes are recruited to endosomes.

Micro-autophagy is also important for organelle homeostasis. Specifically, portions of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) are engulfed into ILVs of late endosomes or lysosomes under conditions that lead to accumulation of excess ER[46](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR46 "Loi, M., Raimondi, A., Morone, D. & Molinari, M. ESCRT-III-driven piecemeal micro-ER-phagy remodels the ER during recovery from ER stress. Nat. Commun. 10, 5058 (2019)."). This ‘micro-ER-phagy’ involves ESCRT-I, ESCRT-III and VPS4, whereas ESCRT-0 is dispensable. A special case of micro-ER-phagy is the degradation of ER exit sites under conditions of nutrient stress, which causes Ca2+ release from lysosomes, ubiquitination of components of the COPII coat in ER exit sites, and their engulfment into ILVs of lysosomes by a process that requires ESCRT-III and ALIX as well as the ALIX-binding Ca2+ sensor ALG2[47](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR47 "Liao, Y. C. et al. COPII with ALG2 and ESCRTs control lysosome-dependent microautophagy of ER exit sites. Dev. Cell 59, 1410–1424.e1414 (2024).").

Under oxidative stress, mitochondria experience a variety of degradative fates, including formation of vesicles derived from the inner mitochondrial membrane (VDIMs), which form herniated extrusions through the outer membrane. VDIMs are engulfed directly by endolysosomal micro-autophagy in a process that involves calcium, ALG2 and the core ESCRTs TSG101, CHMP2 and CHMP4[48](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR48 "Prashar, A. et al. Lysosomes drive the piecemeal removal of mitochondrial inner membrane. Nature 632, 1110–1117 (2024)."). Mechanistically, this response mirrors the dependencies seen for lysosome repair and serves as an alternative to Parkin-dependent mitophagy.

### Cytokinesis

The final stage of cell division, cytokinesis, culminates with the physical separation of the two daughter cells. Abscission requires the whole ESCRT machinery, including components of ESCRT-I, ESCRT-II, ESCRT-III, ALIX and VPS4[49](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR49 "Carlton, J. G. & Martin-Serrano, J. Parallels between cytokinesis and retroviral budding: a role for the ESCRT machinery. Science 316, 1908–1912 (2007)."),[50](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR50 "Morita, E. et al. Human ESCRT and ALIX proteins interact with proteins of the midbody and function in cytokinesis. EMBO J. 26, 4215–4227 (2007). References 49,50 demonstrate the importance of ESCRTs for cytokinetic abscission and show that ALIX and TSG101 are recruited to the midbody by binding to CEP55."). Initially, ESCRT-I and ALIX are recruited to the midbody, an electron-dense structure in the middle of the intercellular bridge, via interaction with midbody proteins such as CEP55 or the centralspindlin complex[49](#ref-CR49 "Carlton, J. G. & Martin-Serrano, J. Parallels between cytokinesis and retroviral budding: a role for the ESCRT machinery. Science 316, 1908–1912 (2007)."),[50](#ref-CR50 "Morita, E. et al. Human ESCRT and ALIX proteins interact with proteins of the midbody and function in cytokinesis. EMBO J. 26, 4215–4227 (2007). References 49,50 demonstrate the importance of ESCRTs for cytokinetic abscission and show that ALIX and TSG101 are recruited to the midbody by binding to CEP55."),[51](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR51 "Lie-Jensen, A. et al. Centralspindlin recruits ALIX to the midbody during cytokinetic abscission in Drosophila via a mechanism analogous to virus budding. Curr. Biol. 29, 3538–3548.e7 (2019)."). This is followed by ESCRT-III recruitment, either via canonical interactions with ESCRT-II or via ALIX[15](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR15 "Christ, L. et al. ALIX and ESCRT-I/II function as parallel ESCRT-III recruiters in cytokinetic abscission. J. Cell Biol. 212, 499–513 (2016)."), and spiral filaments containing ESCRT-III and ALIX extend from the midbody to the abscission site[52](#ref-CR52 "Guizetti, J. et al. Cortical constriction during abscission involves helices of ESCRT-III-dependent filaments. Science 331, 1616–1620 (2011)."),[53](#ref-CR53 "Elia, N., Sougrat, R., Spurlin, T. A., Hurley, J. H. & Lippincott-Schwartz, J. Dynamics of endosomal sorting complex required for transport (ESCRT) machinery during cytokinesis and its role in abscission. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4846–4851 (2011)."),[54](#ref-CR54 "Goliand, I. et al. Resolving ESCRT-III spirals at the intercellular bridge of dividing cells using 3D STORM. Cell Rep. 24, 1756–1764 (2018)."),[55](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR55 "Addi, C. et al. The Flemmingsome reveals an ESCRT-to-membrane coupling via ALIX/syntenin/syndecan-4 required for completion of cytokinesis. Nat. Commun. 11, 1941 (2020)."). The ATPase spastin is recruited by the ESCRT-III component CHMP1B at the abscission site to sever microtubules that traverse the intercellular bridge, and ESCRT-III filaments proceed to accomplish membrane scission[56](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR56 "Mierzwa, B. E. et al. Dynamic subunit turnover in ESCRT-III assemblies is regulated by Vps4 to mediate membrane remodelling during cytokinesis. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 787–798 (2017)."). Association of ESCRT-III filaments to the membrane is facilitated by syntenin, a protein that links the plasma membrane-localized proteoglycan syndecan-4 with ALIX[55](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR55 "Addi, C. et al. The Flemmingsome reveals an ESCRT-to-membrane coupling via ALIX/syntenin/syndecan-4 required for completion of cytokinesis. Nat. Commun. 11, 1941 (2020).").

### Nuclear envelope sealing and quality control

During metazoan cell division, the nuclear envelope is disassembled in prophase to allow segregation of the duplicated chromosomes. New nuclear envelopes start to assemble in anaphase, around the two clusters of chromosomes that form the daughter cell nuclei. These reforming nuclear envelopes are traversed by spindle microtubules that need to be removed to maintain genome integrity. As in cytokinesis, ESCRT-III proteins have a dual role during late anaphase: first, by recruiting spastin, via the ESCRT-III component IST1, to sever spindle microtubules[57](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR57 "Vietri, M. et al. Spastin and ESCRT-III coordinate mitotic spindle disassembly and nuclear envelope sealing. Nature 522, 231–235 (2015)."); and second, by sealing the holes that remain after the microtubule bundles have been dissolved[57](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR57 "Vietri, M. et al. Spastin and ESCRT-III coordinate mitotic spindle disassembly and nuclear envelope sealing. Nature 522, 231–235 (2015)."),[58](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR58 "Olmos, Y., Hodgson, L., Mantell, J., Verkade, P. & Carlton, J. G. ESCRT-III controls nuclear envelope reformation. Nature 522, 236–239 (2015). References 57,58 demonstrate that ESCRT-III mediates closure of holes in the reformed nuclear envelopes during mititoc exit, that CHMP7 recruits ESCRT-III to the reforming nuclear envelope and that the ESCRT-III protein IST1 recruits spastin to sever spindle microtubules."). ESCRT-III is recruited to the microtubule-vacated holes in the nuclear envelope by CHMP7, a protein whose N-terminal part is structurally related to ESCRT-II proteins, whereas its C-terminal part is related to ESCRT-III[57](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR57 "Vietri, M. et al. Spastin and ESCRT-III coordinate mitotic spindle disassembly and nuclear envelope sealing. Nature 522, 231–235 (2015)."). CHMP7 is in turn recruited by binding to the inner nuclear envelope protein LEMD2, which condenses into a liquid-like phase and has been proposed to co-assemble with CHMP7 to form a macromolecular O-ring seal[59](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR59 "Olmos, Y., Perdrix-Rosell, A. & Carlton, J. G. Membrane binding by CHMP7 coordinates ESCRT-III-dependent nuclear envelope reformation. Curr. Biol. 26, 2635–2641 (2016)."),[60](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR60 "von Appen, A. et al. LEM2 phase separation promotes ESCRT-mediated nuclear envelope reformation. Nature 582, 115–118 (2020)."). Studies from yeast have shown that orthologues of LEMD2, CHMP7 and ESCRT-III also participate in quality control of nuclear pore complexes by budding dysfunctional intermediates of nuclear pore complexes into a SINC (storage of improperly assembled nuclear pore complexes) compartment[61](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR61 "Webster, B. M., Colombi, P., Jager, J. & Lusk, C. P. Surveillance of nuclear pore complex assembly by ESCRT-III/Vps4. Cell 159, 388–401 (2014).").

Because of its size and rigidity, the nuclear envelope is prone to damage when cells face physical constraints. Such damage is repaired by ESCRT-III, which is recruited by CHMP7 and LEMD2 by a mechanism that resembles its function during nuclear envelope reformation[62](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR62 "Raab, M. et al. ESCRT III repairs nuclear envelope ruptures during cell migration to limit DNA damage and cell death. Science 352, 359–362 (2016)."),[63](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR63 "Denais, C. M. et al. Nuclear envelope rupture and repair during cancer cell migration. Science 352, 353–358 (2016). References 62,63 demonstrate that ESCRT-III, recruited by CHMP7, repairs the ruptured nuclear envelope."). CHMP7 serves as a sensor of nuclear integrity by being actively transported out of the intact nucleus and recruited by LEMD2 to lesions in the nuclear envelope[64](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR64 "Coyne, A. N. et al. Nuclear accumulation of CHMP7 initiates nuclear pore complex injury and subsequent TDP-43 dysfunction in sporadic and familial ALS. Sci. Transl. Med. 13, eabe1923 (2021)."),[65](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR65 "Vietri, M. et al. Unrestrained ESCRT-III drives micronuclear catastrophe and chromosome fragmentation. Nat. Cell Biol. 22, 856–867 (2020).").

The same surveillance machinery that maintains integrity of the nucleus is recruited to lesions in micronuclei, small nuclear-like compartments that usually contain single chromosomes[65](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR65 "Vietri, M. et al. Unrestrained ESCRT-III drives micronuclear catastrophe and chromosome fragmentation. Nat. Cell Biol. 22, 856–867 (2020)."). Dysregulated ESCRT recruitment to damaged micronuclei may cause micronuclear catastrophe and genome instability, as discussed below.

### Plasma membrane repair

Like the nuclear envelope, the plasma membrane is exposed to physical stress, and can be a target for toxins and chemical stressors. ESCRT proteins have a central role in multiple mechanisms for maintaining plasma membrane integrity. Influx of Ca2+ through damaged sites is sensed by the Ca2+-binding protein ALG2, which provides a probable mechanism for ESCRT-III recruitment via its binding to ALIX[66](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR66 "Shukla, S., Larsen, K. P., Ou, C., Rose, K. & Hurley, J. H. In vitro reconstitution of calcium-dependent recruitment of the human ESCRT machinery in lysosomal membrane repair. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 119, e2205590119 (2022)."). There is evidence of increased ectosome formation at sites of plasma membrane damage, suggesting that ESCRTs mediate plasma membrane repair by promoting shedding of damaged membrane areas[67](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR67 "Jimenez, A. J. et al. ESCRT machinery is required for plasma membrane repair. Science 343, 1247136 (2014). This study demonstrates that ESCRTs are recruited to wounds in the plasma membrane to mediate their repair, and that extracellular buds are detected at the sites of ESCRT recruitment.").

### Lysosome repair and macroautophagy

Lysosomes accumulate a number of components that can potentially harm the membrane, and there are multiple mechanisms for repair of the lysosome membrane, including ESCRT-mediated repair[68](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR68 "Skowyra, M. L., Schlesinger, P. H., Naismith, T. V. & Hanson, P. I. Triggered recruitment of ESCRT machinery promotes endolysosomal repair. Science 360, eaar5078 (2018)."),[69](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR69 "Radulovic, M. et al. ESCRT‐mediated lysosome repair precedes lysophagy and promotes cell survival. EMBO J. 37, e99753 (2018). References 68,69 show that Ca2+ release from damaged lysosomes triggers ESCRT recruitment, which mediates their repair and promotes cell viability."). Except for ESCRT-0, all canonical ESCRT components are rapidly recruited to damaged lysosomes[69](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR69 "Radulovic, M. et al. ESCRT‐mediated lysosome repair precedes lysophagy and promotes cell survival. EMBO J. 37, e99753 (2018). References 68,69 show that Ca2+ release from damaged lysosomes triggers ESCRT recruitment, which mediates their repair and promotes cell viability."), probably triggered by Ca2+ release through lesions in their membrane or by Ca2+ channels activated by luminal deacidification[68](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR68 "Skowyra, M. L., Schlesinger, P. H., Naismith, T. V. & Hanson, P. I. Triggered recruitment of ESCRT machinery promotes endolysosomal repair. Science 360, eaar5078 (2018)."). The Ca2+-binding protein ALG2 is recruited to damaged lysosomes and is essential for repair of osmotically challenged lysosomes, although additional Ca2+ sensors are likely to be involved under other conditions of damage[68](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR68 "Skowyra, M. L., Schlesinger, P. H., Naismith, T. V. & Hanson, P. I. Triggered recruitment of ESCRT machinery promotes endolysosomal repair. Science 360, eaar5078 (2018)."),[70](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR70 "Chen, W., Motsinger, M. M., Li, J., Bohannon, K. P. & Hanson, P. I. Ca2+-sensor ALG-2 engages ESCRTs to enhance lysosomal membrane resilience to osmotic stress. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 121, e2318412121 (2024)."). ALG2 binds to ALIX and the ESCRT-I subunit TSG101[71](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR71 "Katoh, K. et al. The penta-EF-hand protein ALG-2 interacts directly with the ESCRT-I component TSG101, and Ca2+-dependently co-localizes to aberrant endosomes with dominant-negative AAA ATPase SKD1/Vps4B. Biochem. J 391, 677–685 (2005)."),[72](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR72 "Missotten, M., Nichols, A., Rieger, K. & Sadoul, R. Alix, a novel mouse protein undergoing calcium-dependent interaction with the apoptosis-linked-gene 2 (ALG-2) protein. Cell Death Differ. 6, 124–129 (1999)."), which mediate recruitment of ESCRT-III to damaged lysosomes[68](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR68 "Skowyra, M. L., Schlesinger, P. H., Naismith, T. V. & Hanson, P. I. Triggered recruitment of ESCRT machinery promotes endolysosomal repair. Science 360, eaar5078 (2018)."),[69](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR69 "Radulovic, M. et al. ESCRT‐mediated lysosome repair precedes lysophagy and promotes cell survival. EMBO J. 37, e99753 (2018). References 68,69 show that Ca2+ release from damaged lysosomes triggers ESCRT recruitment, which mediates their repair and promotes cell viability."). An alternative mechanism for ESCRT-III recruitment is provided by LRRK2, a protein kinase that is activated by lysosome damage to phosphorylate the small GTPase RAB8A, which triggers recruitment of ESCRT-III[73](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR73 "Herbst, S. et al. LRRK2 activation controls the repair of damaged endomembranes in macrophages. EMBO J. 39, e104494 (2020)."). The exact mechanism of ESCRT-mediated lysosome repair remains unknown, but inward budding of damaged areas is an attractive possibility.

If lysosome damage is too extensive to be repaired, the damaged lysosome becomes sequestered by autophagic membranes and degraded by a macroautophagic process known as lysophagy[74](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR74 "Hung, Y. H., Chen, L. M., Yang, J. Y. & Yang, W. Y. Spatiotemporally controlled induction of autophagy-mediated lysosome turnover. Nat. Commun. 4, 2111 (2013)."),[75](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR75 "Maejima, I. et al. Autophagy sequesters damaged lysosomes to control lysosomal biogenesis and kidney injury. EMBO J. 32, 2336–2347 (2013)."). A link between ESCRT-mediated repair and lysophagy is provided by spartin, which binds coincidently to the ESCRT-III component IST1 and regions of disordered lipids in the lysosome membrane. Spartin in turn recruits the ubiquitin E3 ligase ITCH, which marks the damaged lysosome with Lys63-linked ubiquitin chains to trigger lysophagy[76](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR76 "Gahlot, P. et al. Lysosomal damage sensing and lysophagy initiation by SPG20-ITCH. Mol. Cell 84, 1556–1569 e1510 (2024).").

Autophagosomes are formed from phagophores, cup-shaped membrane cisternae that expand and eventually close to form double-membrane structures[77](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR77 "Vargas, J. N. S., Hamasaki, M., Kawabata, T., Youle, R. J. & Yoshimori, T. The mechanisms and roles of selective autophagy in mammals. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 24, 167–185 (2023)."). The final closure of the autophagosome is mediated by ESCRT-III and requires ESCRT-I[78](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR78 "Takahashi, Y. et al. An autophagy assay reveals the ESCRT-III component CHMP2A as a regulator of phagophore closure. Nat. Commun. 9, 2855 (2018)."),[79](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR79 "Zhen, Y. et al. ESCRT-mediated phagophore sealing during mitophagy. Autophagy 16, 826–841 (2020). References 78,79 show that ESCRT-I and ESCRT-III mediate closure of the autophagosome and that this promotes autophagic flux."). Sensing of the hole is mediated by the ESCRT-I subunit VPS37A, which contains an N-terminal region that senses high membrane curvature[80](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR80 "Ye, Y. et al. Identification of membrane curvature sensing motifs essential for VPS37A phagophore recruitment and autophagosome closure. Commun. Biol. 7, 334 (2024)."). Interference with ESCRT-mediated autophagosome sealing inhibits autophagy, presumably by preventing fusion of the autophagosome with the lysosome[79](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR79 "Zhen, Y. et al. ESCRT-mediated phagophore sealing during mitophagy. Autophagy 16, 826–841 (2020). References 78,79 show that ESCRT-I and ESCRT-III mediate closure of the autophagosome and that this promotes autophagic flux."). Autophagosomes are maintained in a sealed state through interactions between ESCRT components and ATG8 family proteins in the autophagosome membrane[81](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR81 "Javed, R. et al. Mammalian ATG8 proteins maintain autophagosomal membrane integrity through ESCRTs. EMBO J. 42, e112845 (2023)."). Given the influence of ESCRTs on cell signalling, which is known to control autophagy[82](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR82 "Klionsky, D. J. et al. Autophagy in major human diseases. EMBO J. 40, e108863 (2021)."), ESCRT defects might also affect autophagy by additional mechanisms.

### Non-canonical ESCRT functions

Non-canonical functions have been described for some ESCRT components, including ESCRT-II in mRNA localization, CHMP1 in chromatin organization and CHMP1B and IST1 in mediating fatty acid transport from lipid droplets to peroxisomes[83](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR83 "Vietri, M., Radulovic, M. & Stenmark, H. The many functions of ESCRTs. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 21, 25–42 (2020)."). This indicates that some ESCRT proteins may have additional functions that are unrelated to membrane severing.

## ESCRTs in infection, inflammation and immunity

### Viral budding and ectosome biogenesis

HIV-1 and many other membrane-enveloped viruses bud directly from the plasma membrane in an ESCRT-dependent manner[84](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR84 "Votteler, J. & Sundquist, W. I. Virus budding and the ESCRT pathway. Cell Host Microbe 14, 232–241 (2013).") (Fig. [2](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig2)). The primary mode of ESCRT recruitment to HIV-1 and Ebola virus budding site is via a PTAP ‘late domain’ sequence in the viral Gag protein, which binds to the ESCRT-I subunit TSG101[85](#ref-CR85 "VerPlank, L. et al. Tsg101, a homologue of ubiquitin-conjugating (E2) enzymes, binds the L domain in HIV type 1 Pr55Gag. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 98, 7724–7729 (2001)."),[86](#ref-CR86 "Garrus, J. E. et al. Tsg101 and the vacuolar protein sorting pathway are essential for HIV-1 budding. Cell 107, 55–65 (2001)."),[87](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR87 "Martin-Serrano, J., Zang, T. & Bieniasz, P. D. HIV-1 and Ebola virus encode small peptide motifs that recruit Tsg101 to sites of particle assembly to facilitate egress. Nat. Med. 7, 1313–1319 (2001). References 85–87 demonstrate that the ESCRT-I subunit TSG101, through interaction with a PTAP motif in viral Gag proteins, mediates viral budding."). The ALIX branch of the ESCRT pathway also contributes by interacting with YPXL[13](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR13 "Strack, B., Calistri, A., Craig, S., Popova, E. & Gottlinger, H. G. AIP1/ALIX is a binding partner for HIV-1 p6 and EIAV p9 functioning in virus budding. Cell 114, 689–699 (2003)."),[14](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR14 "von Schwedler, U. K. et al. The protein network of HIV budding. Cell 114, 701–713 (2003)."), another late domain sequence. The kinetics of ESCRT recruitment to HIV-1 budding sites are consistent with the biophysical models for membrane scission[88](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR88 "Johnson, D. S., Bleck, M. & Simon, S. M. Timing of ESCRT-III protein recruitment and membrane scission during HIV-1 assembly. eLife 7, e36221 (2018)."). ESCRT mediation of viral budding is thought to have been co-opted from the host process of extracellular vesicle budding[89](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR89 "Jackson, C. E., Scruggs, B. S., Schaffer, J. E. & Hanson, P. I. Effects of inhibiting VPS4 support a general role for ESCRTs in extracellular vesicle biogenesis. Biophys. J. 113, 1342–1352 (2017)."). ESCRTs are also required for the formation of various intracellular compartments involved in viral replication[83](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR83 "Vietri, M., Radulovic, M. & Stenmark, H. The many functions of ESCRTs. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 21, 25–42 (2020)."). The ESCRT–plasma membrane budding pathway has been harnessed to accelerate production of artificial ectosomes bearing therapeutic cargoes, such as SARS-CoV-2 spike-bearing virus-like particles, in response to mRNA-based vaccination[90](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR90 "Hoffmann, M. A. G. et al. ESCRT recruitment to SARS-CoV-2 spike induces virus-like particles that improve mRNA vaccines. Cell 186, 2380–2391.e2389 (2023).").

### Immune functions of ESCRTs related to membrane repair

Owing to their membrane repair abilities (Fig. [2](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig2)), ESCRTs are cytoprotective against immunogenic cell death pathways, such as necroptosis, pyroptosis and ferroptosis. Although they are mediated by different stimuli and upstream signalling complexes, these death pathways all result in the formation of protein-based pores in the plasma membrane that release intracellular content, triggering an immune response (Fig. [3](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig3)).

**Fig. 3: ESCRTs in immunity and cancer.**

![Fig. 3: ESCRTs in immunity and cancer.](//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-025-08950-y/MediaObjects/41586_2025_8950_Fig3_HTML.png)

[Full size image](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y/figures/3)

Functions in inflammation and immunity stem from the roles of ESCRTs in membrane trafficking, including viral budding, controlling inflammatory signalling by TNF receptors and interferon receptors in MVEs, and terminating cGAS–STING signalling in lysosomes, or in membrane repair, including protection against cell death and counteracting damage by exogenous pore-forming proteins. ESCRT functions in cancer stem from aberrant secretion of exosomes (leading to dysregulated anti-tumour immunity), abnormal cytokinesis and perturbed nuclear and micronuclear envelope (contributing to genetic instability of tumours), altered trafficking and signalling of growth factor receptors in endosomes. Moreover, cancer cells can exhibit deletions or altered expression of ESCRT-encoding genes.

In necroptosis, phosphorylation of the pseudokinase MLKL causes its oligomerization and pore formation in the plasma membrane, leading to membrane permeabilization. ESCRT activity can counteract necroptosis by two parallel mechanisms. First, ESCRT-III components recruited to the plasma membrane drive shedding of broken membrane fragments as described above, thus repairing the damage and prolonging cell survival[91](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR91 "Gong, Y. N. et al. ESCRT-III acts downstream of MLKL to regulate necroptotic cell death and its consequences. Cell 169, 286–300.e216 (2017)."). Second, ESCRT-I activity on endosomes mediates generation of exosomes that incorporate phospho-MLKL, thus exporting this pore-forming protein out of cells[92](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR92 "Yoon, S., Kovalenko, A., Bogdanov, K. & Wallach, D. MLKL, the protein that mediates necroptosis, also regulates endosomal trafficking and extracellular vesicle generation. Immunity 47, 51–65 e57 (2017)."). In pyroptosis, activation of the inflammatory caspases 1, 4 and 5 causes cleavage and activation of gasdermin D, which forms pores in the plasma membrane that release inflammatory cytokines such as IL-1β and IL-18. Similarly to necroptosis, ESCRT-III components mediate removal of gasdermin D pores by membrane shedding[93](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR93 "Ruhl, S. et al. ESCRT-dependent membrane repair negatively regulates pyroptosis downstream of GSDMD activation. Science 362, 956–960 (2018)."), and ESCRT-I promotes packaging of gasdermin D and Il-1β into exosomes[94](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR94 "Liao, Y. et al. The Ras GTPase-activating-like protein IQGAP1 bridges Gasdermin D to the ESCRT system to promote IL-1β release via exosomes. EMBO J. 42, e110780 (2023)."). In both necroptosis and pyroptosis, recruitment of ESCRT components to the perforated plasma membrane occurs following influx of extracellular Ca2+ through membrane pores. The autophagy-associated lipid scramblase ATG9A and its interacting protein IQGAP1 cooperate with ESCRTs in protection of the plasma membrane against necroptotic and pyroptotic damage[95](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR95 "Claude-Taupin, A. et al. ATG9A protects the plasma membrane from programmed and incidental permeabilization. Nat. Cell Biol. 23, 846–858 (2021)."). Finally, in ferroptosis, membrane permeabilization and the resulting Ca2+ influx are induced by iron-driven lipid peroxidation and counteracted by ESCRT-III-dependent repair[96](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR96 "Pedrera, L. et al. Ferroptotic pores induce Ca2+ fluxes and ESCRT-III activation to modulate cell death kinetics. Cell Death Differ. 28, 1644–1657 (2021)."),[97](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR97 "Dai, E., Meng, L., Kang, R., Wang, X. & Tang, D. ESCRT-III-dependent membrane repair blocks ferroptosis. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 522, 415–421 (2020).").

ESCRT activity can also restrain membrane damage caused by exogenous pore-forming proteins delivered by immune cells or derived from bacterial pathogens. In the first case, ESCRTs limit plasma membrane permeabilization of tumour cells induced by perforin, which is provided by CD8+ cytotoxic T cells through the immunological synapse formed between the two cell types[98](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR98 "Ritter, A. T. et al. ESCRT-mediated membrane repair protects tumor-derived cells against T cell attack. Science 376, 377–382 (2022). This study shows that ESCRT-dependent membrane repair needs to be inhibited in cancer cells for their efficient elimination by cytotoxic T cells."). This cytoprotective role of ESCRTs for cancer cells is detrimental at the organismal level, as it diminishes the anti-tumour response.

In the case of pathogen-derived pore-forming toxins, such as the *Staphylococcus aureus* α-toxin, ESCRT components are recruited to endolysosomes by the interaction with the lipopolysaccharide-induced tumour necrosis factor LITAF, which is triggered by K+ influx. This stimulates biogenesis of exosomes containing perforated membranes and their discharge by secretion, thus limiting host cell death[99](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR99 "Stefani, C. et al. LITAF protects against pore-forming protein-induced cell death by promoting membrane repair. Sci. Immunol. 9, eabq6541 (2024).").

Modulation of immunogenic cell death represents a broad therapeutic target in oncology and infectious diseases, as tumour cells and pathogens often develop mechanisms to inhibit release of alarming signals and thus escape immunosurveillance. As proofs-of-concept for potential therapeutic approaches, inducing gasdermin-dependent pyroptosis with concomitant blocking of ESCRT-mediated membrane repair has been shown to stimulate anti-tumour immune responses[100](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR100 "Li, Z. et al. Enhancing Gasdermin-induced tumor pyroptosis through preventing ESCRT-dependent cell membrane repair augments antitumor immune response. Nat. Commun. 13, 6321 (2022). This proof-of-concept study demonstrates the feasibility of concomitant induction of pyroptosis and inhibition of ESCRT-mediated membrane repair for in vivo anti-tumour therapies."), and transient overexpression of the ESCRT-III subunit CHMP4B suppressed senescence of human fibroblasts that was induced by plasma membrane damage[101](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR101 "Suda, K. et al. Plasma membrane damage limits re plicative lifespan in yeast and induces premature senescence in human fibroblasts. Nat. Aging 4, 319–335 (2024)."). Any manipulation of ESCRT activity in tumours has to be balanced against effects in normal cells and tissues. For example, rupture of endolysosomes in conventional dendritic cells has been proposed to release internalized antigens into the cytoplasm for cross-presentation on major histocompatibility protein class I molecules to activate CD8+ T cells. By restoring the integrity of endolysosomes, ESCRT-III reduces the efficiency of this process, which is crucial for adaptive immunity against pathogens[102](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR102 "Gros, M. et al. Endocytic membrane repair by ESCRT-III controls antigen export to the cytosol during antigen cross-presentation. Cell Rep. 40, 111205 (2022).").

### Immune functions of ESCRTs related to membrane trafficking

Since ESCRTs participate in trafficking of receptors and mediators of immune pathways, mislocalization of ESCRT-dependent signalling cargoes causes various inflammatory phenotypes. For example, ESCRT-I or CHMP4B depletion causes endosomal accumulation and ligand-independent activation of cytokine receptors of the TNF receptor superfamily that leads to their ligand-independent activation of NF-κB-dependent sterile inflammation[103](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR103 "Maminska, A. et al. ESCRT proteins restrict constitutive NF-κB signaling by trafficking cytokine receptors. Sci. Signal. 9, ra8 (2016)."). Such endosome-derived inflammatory signalling eventually results in cell death by apoptosis, necroptosis, and pyroptosis, as demonstrated for acute myeloid leukaemia cells lacking PTPN23[104](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR104 "Song, D. et al. PTPN23-dependent ESCRT machinery functions as a cell death checkpoint. Nat. Commun. 15, 10364 (2024).") (also known as HD-PTP). Trafficking of interferon-α/β receptor between the plasma membrane and endosomes mediated by ESCRT-0 components HRS (also known as HGS) and STAM provides spatiotemporal control of JAK–STAT signalling that is induced differently by interferon-α and interferon-β[105](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR105 "Zanin, N. et al. STAM and Hrs interact sequentially with IFN-α receptor to control spatiotemporal JAK–STAT endosomal activation. Nat. Cell Biol. 25, 425–438 (2023)."). ESCRT-0 and ESCRT-I components and clathrin are necessary for proper trafficking of activated T cell receptors into synaptic ectosomes in the immunological synapse between T cells and antigen-presenting cells[106](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR106 "Kvalvaag, A. et al. Clathrin mediates both internalization and vesicular release of triggered T cell receptor at the immunological synapse. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 120, e2211368120 (2023)."). Similarly, ESCRT-dependent trafficking regulates strength and duration of signalling by CD40, a receptor that is crucial for B cell development[107](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR107 "Jiang, C. et al. CRISPR/Cas9 screens reveal multiple layers of B cell CD40 regulation. Cell Rep. 28, 1307–1322.e1308 (2019).").

Recent studies have identified the role of ESCRTs in the degradation of STING and termination of its signalling function[108](#ref-CR108 "Kuchitsu, Y. et al. STING signalling is terminated through ESCRT-dependent microautophagy of vesicles originating from recycling endosomes. Nat. Cell Biol. 25, 453–466 (2023)."),[109](#ref-CR109 "Gentili, M. et al. ESCRT-dependent STING degradation inhibits steady-state and cGAMP-induced signalling. Nat. Commun. 14, 611 (2023)."),[110](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR110 "Balka, K. R. et al. Termination of STING responses is mediated via ESCRT-dependent degradation. EMBO J. 42, e112712 (2023). References 108–110 identify the role and mechanism of ESCRT-dependent degradation in termination of cGAS–STING signalling."). STING is an ER-resident transmembrane protein that is essential for innate immunity against cytoplasmic DNA. Activation of cyclic GMP–AMP synthase (cGAS) results in production of the 2′3′-cyclic GMP–AMP (cGAMP) ligand that activates STING. STING then translocates to the Golgi apparatus, where it signals to activate the transcription factors IRF3 and NF-κB, leading to production of type I interferons and pro-inflammatory cytokines[111](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR111 "Zhang, Z. et al. Multifaceted functions of STING in human health and disease: from molecular mechanism to targeted strategy. Signal Transduct. Target. Ther. 7, 394 (2022)."). Termination of STING signalling was first identified to involve packaging of STING into post-Golgi, AP-1 positive, clathrin-coated vesicles to ferry STING for lysosomal degradation[112](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR112 "Liu, Y. et al. Clathrin-associated AP-1 controls termination of STING signalling. Nature 610, 761–767 (2022)."). Subsequent studies revealed an ESCRT-dependent mechanism for engulfment of whole STING-containing vesicles by micro-autophagy. Specifically, ESCRT-0 and ESCRT-I components recognize ubiquitinated STING and encapsulate STING-bearing vesicles into LAMP1-positive compartments for degradation[108](#ref-CR108 "Kuchitsu, Y. et al. STING signalling is terminated through ESCRT-dependent microautophagy of vesicles originating from recycling endosomes. Nat. Cell Biol. 25, 453–466 (2023)."),[109](#ref-CR109 "Gentili, M. et al. ESCRT-dependent STING degradation inhibits steady-state and cGAMP-induced signalling. Nat. Commun. 14, 611 (2023)."),[110](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR110 "Balka, K. R. et al. Termination of STING responses is mediated via ESCRT-dependent degradation. EMBO J. 42, e112712 (2023). References 108–110 identify the role and mechanism of ESCRT-dependent degradation in termination of cGAS–STING signalling."). Consistently, depletion of ESCRTs causes accumulation of STING vesicles in the cytoplasm and a prolonged interferon response. Extensive efforts are underway to understand the role of ESCRTs in the regulation of STING-mediated responses because aberrant STING signalling is observed in autoimmune diseases, such as STING-associated vasculopathy with onset in infancy, Aicardi–Goutières syndrome, COPA syndrome, Niemann–Pick disease type C, systemic lupus erythematosus and rheumatoid arthritis, as well as many neurological, cardiovascular, metabolic and digestive tract disorders[111](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR111 "Zhang, Z. et al. Multifaceted functions of STING in human health and disease: from molecular mechanism to targeted strategy. Signal Transduct. Target. Ther. 7, 394 (2022).").

## ESCRTs in cancer

Although ESCRT components do not seem to directly drive oncogenesis, several of the physiological ESCRT functions, when disrupted, predispose to carcinogenesis or affect disease progression (Fig. [3](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig3)). Perturbed activity of ESCRTs in cytokinesis and nuclear and micronuclear envelope maintenance (Fig. [2](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig2)) can favour genetic instability of cancer cells, and aberrant production and cargo loading of exosomes or other trafficking defects may contribute to impaired downregulation of growth factor receptors, dysregulated anti-tumour immune responses and tumour spread. The genetic changes or altered expression of ESCRT components observed in some human cancers may thus represent therapeutic vulnerabilities for selective elimination of cancer cells.

### ESCRT functions in tumour genetic instability

The ESCRT-III component CHMP4C is a key factor in the abscission checkpoint that halts cytokinetic abscission when chromatin is detected in the intercellular bridge[113](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR113 "Carlton, J. G., Caballe, A., Agromayor, M., Kloc, M. & Martin-Serrano, J. ESCRT-III governs the Aurora B-mediated abscission checkpoint through CHMP4C. Science 336, 220–225 (2012)."). A polymorphism in human CHMP4C T232 increases susceptibility to ovarian cancer owing to impaired interaction of CHMP4C T232 with ALIX, which dysregulates the abscission checkpoint and sensitizes cells to genotoxic stressors[114](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR114 "Sadler, J. B. A. et al. A cancer-associated polymorphism in ESCRT-III disrupts the abscission checkpoint and promotes genome instability. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E8900–E8908 (2018)."). However, most reports that link ESCRTs to maintenance of genome integrity focus on their role in repair of nuclear and micronuclear envelopes[62](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR62 "Raab, M. et al. ESCRT III repairs nuclear envelope ruptures during cell migration to limit DNA damage and cell death. Science 352, 359–362 (2016)."),[63](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR63 "Denais, C. M. et al. Nuclear envelope rupture and repair during cancer cell migration. Science 352, 353–358 (2016). References 62,63 demonstrate that ESCRT-III, recruited by CHMP7, repairs the ruptured nuclear envelope."). Shattering of single chromosomes (chromothripsis) in cancer cells can result from irreversible rupture of micronuclei. The resulting release of DNA fragments into the cytoplasm favours further genetic and epigenetic alterations that along with cGAS–STING-mediated inflammatory signalling drive cancer progression and metastasis. Notably, breakage of micronuclei is driven by the same ESCRT machinery, including the CHMP7–LEMD2 module that normally repairs the nuclear envelope. However, unrestrained and excessive accumulation of CHMP7–LEMD2 in micronuclei drives their collapse[65](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR65 "Vietri, M. et al. Unrestrained ESCRT-III drives micronuclear catastrophe and chromosome fragmentation. Nat. Cell Biol. 22, 856–867 (2020)."). Furthermore, mitochondria-generated reactive oxygen species (ROS) and the autophagy receptor SQSTM1 prevent ESCRT-mediated repair of micronuclear membrane[115](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR115 "Martin, S. et al. A p62-dependent rheostat dictates micronuclei catastrophe and chromosome rearrangements. Science 385, eadj7446 (2024)."),[116](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR116 "Di Bona, M. et al. Micronuclear collapse from oxidative damage. Science 385, eadj8691 (2024). References 115,116 explain the molecular mechanisms underlying destabilization of micronuclei, which involve reactive oxygen species and p62, as well as inhibition of ESCRT-mediated repair of micronuclear membranes."). ROS induces oligomerization and aggregation of CHMP7–LEMD2 in the micronuclei that contributes to membrane destabilization. Additionally, ROS-driven oligomerization of SQSTM1 and its micronuclear accumulation promotes autophagic degradation of the reparatory ESCRT-III subunits CHMP4B, CHMP2A and CHMP7. Consistently, hypoxic ovarian or head and neck cancers exhibit high numbers of CHMP7-positive ruptured micronuclei, and SQSTM1 expression levels correlate with increased chromothripsis and are linked to poor prognosis in colorectal cancer[115](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR115 "Martin, S. et al. A p62-dependent rheostat dictates micronuclei catastrophe and chromosome rearrangements. Science 385, eadj7446 (2024)."),[116](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR116 "Di Bona, M. et al. Micronuclear collapse from oxidative damage. Science 385, eadj8691 (2024). References 115,116 explain the molecular mechanisms underlying destabilization of micronuclei, which involve reactive oxygen species and p62, as well as inhibition of ESCRT-mediated repair of micronuclear membranes.").

### Functions of ESCRTs related to anti-tumour immunity

ESCRT-mediated membrane trafficking and repair can profoundly affect the ability of cancer cells to be recognized and eliminated by immune cells. Some ESCRT components contribute to trafficking or exosomal secretion of PD-L1, a tumour-derived immunosuppressive transmembrane ligand. ERK-mediated phosphorylation of the ESCRT-0 protein HRS stimulates incorporation of PD-L1 into exosomes that upon secretion inhibit migration of CD8+ T cells into melanoma tumours[117](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR117 "Guan, L. et al. HRS phosphorylation drives immunosuppressive exosome secretion and restricts CD8+ T-cell infiltration into tumors. Nat. Commun. 13, 4078 (2022)."). Loss of the ESCRT-I subunit VPS37A, the only one of the four VPS37 isoforms that is involved in autophagosome sealing, upregulates PD-L1 by transcriptional activation mediated by STAT3 in breast and ovarian cancer cells[118](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR118 "Tomasich, E. et al. Loss of HCRP1 leads to upregulation of PD-L1 via STAT3 activation and is of prognostic significance in EGFR-dependent cancer. Transl. Res. 230, 21–33 (2021)."). The amount of ALIX is inversely correlated with PD-L1 on the surface of EGFR-dependent breast cancer cells and thus with the extent of immunosuppression[119](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR119 "Monypenny, J. et al. ALIX regulates tumor-mediated immunosuppression by controlling EGFR activity and PD-L1 presentation. Cell Rep. 24, 630–641 (2018)."). The ESCRT-III protein CHMP2A increases resistance of glioblastoma stem cells and head and neck squamous carcinoma cells to elimination by natural killer cells[120](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR120 "Bernareggi, D. et al. CHMP2A regulates tumor sensitivity to natural killer cell-mediated cytotoxicity. Nat. Commun. 13, 1899 (2022)."). Mechanistically, CHMP2A, presumably via its canonical function in ESCRT-III, promotes secretion of extracellular vesicles that contain MICA, MICB and TRAIL ligands, which in turn induce apoptosis of natural killer cells.

### Deficiencies of ESCRTs in cancer cells

Some ESCRT components are lost in human cancers owing to large chromosomal deletions. Such deletions can result in the loss of tumour suppressor genes and may also affect genes that do not directly contribute to cancer development—these latter defects are known as passenger mutations. An example of deletion of an ESCRT gene leading to cancer development is the gene encoding the ESCRT-associated ALIX-related protein PTPN23, a haploinsufficient tumour suppressor that is frequently lost in lung cancer due to the deletion of chromosome 3p21.3, which correlates with poor survival[121](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR121 "Manteghi, S. et al. Haploinsufficiency of the ESCRT component HD-PTP predisposes to cancer. Cell Rep. 15, 1893–1900 (2016)."). Homozygous deletion of *Ptpn23* is embryonic lethal in mice, whereas loss of one allele promotes spontaneous tumour formation and lymphomagenesis owing to integrin-dependent B cell lymphoma survival and invasion.

In contrast to *PTPN23*, loss of *VPS4B* due to the deletion of chromosome 18q or loss of *VPS4A* on chromosome 16q, which is observed in many cancer types, is an example of a passenger mutation[122](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR122 "Szymanska, E. et al. Synthetic lethality between VPS4A and VPS4B triggers an inflammatory response in colorectal cancer. EMBO Mol. Med. 12, e10812 (2020)."),[123](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR123 "Neggers, J. E. et al. Synthetic lethal interaction between the ESCRT paralog enzymes VPS4A and VPS4B in cancers harboring loss of chromosome 18q or 16q. Cell Rep. 33, 108493 (2020). References 122,123 uncover the molecular basis of synthetic lethality between VPS4A and VPS4B, and its possible application in anti-cancer therapies."). Cells that are deficient for one paralogue survive because the second paralogue provides an ATPase function that is critical for multiple ESCRT activities, whereas loss of both paralogues results in synthetic lethality. Cells depleted of both paralogues activate NF-κB signalling and sterile inflammation, leading to subsequent immunogenic cell death and the release of intracellular content, driving activation of anti-tumour macrophages[122](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR122 "Szymanska, E. et al. Synthetic lethality between VPS4A and VPS4B triggers an inflammatory response in colorectal cancer. EMBO Mol. Med. 12, e10812 (2020).").

Other ESCRT subunits are also altered in tumours—for instance, expression of *VPS37A* and *VPS37B* is decreased in liver, colorectal and ovarian cancers[124](#ref-CR124 "Xu, Z., Liang, L., Wang, H., Li, T. & Zhao, M. HCRP1, a novel gene that is downregulated in hepatocellular carcinoma, encodes a growth-inhibitory protein. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 311, 1057–1066 (2003)."),[125](#ref-CR125 "Wittinger, M. et al. hVps37A status affects prognosis and cetuximab sensitivity in ovarian cancer. Clin. Cancer Res. 17, 7816–7827 (2011)."),[126](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR126 "Kolmus, K. et al. Concurrent depletion of Vps37 proteins evokes ESCRT-I destabilization and profound cellular stress responses. J. Cell Sci. 134, jcs250951 (2021)."). Their concomitant depletion in vitro induces NF-κB-dependent inflammatory signalling, similarly to loss of both *VPS4A* and *VPS4B*, and inhibits cell proliferation but promotes cell migration[126](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR126 "Kolmus, K. et al. Concurrent depletion of Vps37 proteins evokes ESCRT-I destabilization and profound cellular stress responses. J. Cell Sci. 134, jcs250951 (2021)."),[127](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR127 "Migliano, S. M. et al. Removal of hypersignaling endosomes by simaphagy. Autophagy 20, 769–791 (2024)."). Incomplete ESCRT function in endosomal sorting causes accumulation of ubiquitinated signalling receptors in the endosome membrane, which triggers autophagy of hypersignalling endosomes (simaphagy)[127](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR127 "Migliano, S. M. et al. Removal of hypersignaling endosomes by simaphagy. Autophagy 20, 769–791 (2024)."). Since VPS37A mediates autophagosome closure[128](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR128 "Takahashi, Y. et al. VPS37A directs ESCRT recruitment for phagophore closure. J. Cell Biol. 218, 3336–3354 (2019)."), simaphagy is stalled in cells that lack VPS37A, and this could contribute to the malignant phenotype.

Inactivation of some ESCRT components that are relevant to oncogenesis causes additional, metabolism-related phenotypes. Mice with reduced PTPN23 expression exhibit lipodystrophy and alterations in the adipose tissue[129](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR129 "Schultz, D. F. et al. Loss of HD-PTP function results in lipodystrophy, defective cellular signaling and altered lipid homeostasis. J. Cell Sci. 137, jcs262032 (2024)."), whereas VPS37A regulates glucagon signalling in the liver, and its depletion causes hyperglycaemia and insulin resistance[130](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR130 "Sekar, R. et al. Vps37a regulates hepatic glucose production by controlling glucagon receptor localization to endosomes. Cell Metab. 34, 2047 (2022)."), possibly owing to the canonical functions of ESCRTs in endosomal receptor sorting. However, VPS4A was recently identified as a lipophagy receptor with reduced expression in human steatotic livers[131](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR131 "Das, D. et al. VPS4A is the selective receptor for lipophagy in mice and humans. Mol. Cell 84, 4436–4453.e8 (2024)."), suggesting an additional non-canonical function for this ESCRT-associated ATPase.

## ESCRTs in neurological diseases

The essential roles of ESCRTs in neural functions is underscored by the spectrum of rare genetic mutations in ESCRT genes that give rise to neurodegenerative and neurodevelopmental diseases (Fig. [4](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Fig4) and Table [1](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#Tab1)).

**Fig. 4: Overview of pathologic events linked to ESCRT proteins or ESCRT pathway function in neurodegenerative diseases.**

![Fig. 4: Overview of pathologic events linked to ESCRT proteins or ESCRT pathway function in neurodegenerative diseases.](//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-025-08950-y/MediaObjects/41586_2025_8950_Fig4_HTML.png)

[Full size image](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y/figures/4)

**a**, Disruptions in neuronal branching and synaptic function. Mutations in *CHMP2B* associated with FTD and ALS cause alterations to synaptic vesicle cycling and neuronal arborization. **b**, Nuclear membrane invagination. Although the mechanisms remain unclear, altered nuclear membrane morphology has been observed in *C9orf72*-associated ALS/FTD. In tauopathy, peri-nuclear phosophorylated tau accumulations result in altered nuclear membrane morphology. **c**, Altered nuclear pore complex homeostasis. In sporadic ALS and *C9orf72*-associated ALS/FTD, CHMP7 accumulates in the nucleus, leading to alterations in nuclear pore complex composition. In turn, nuclear pore complex deficiencies at least in part contribute to TDP-43 nuclear loss of function and mislocalizaiton observed in disease. **d**, Impaired protein degradation. Abnormal cytoplasmic protein aggregates are observed in all neurodegenerative diseases. **e**, Pathologic protein spreading. Tau and α-synuclein aggregates can be delivered to neighbouring cells through exosomal release, resulting in spread of pathology. **f**, Malformed MVEs. Mutations in *CHMP2B* associated with FTD and ALS result in the malformation of MVBs that are unable to fuse with lysosomes, ultimately resulting in impaired protein degradation and accumulation of lipids.

### ESCRT genes in neurodegenerative disease

The strongest evidence implicating ESCRT proteins in neurodegenerative disease is the finding that genetic mutations in the ESCRT-III protein CHMP2B are causative of a small subset of frontotemporal dementia (FTD) and amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS). These autosomal dominant mutations cause defect in multiple structural and functional domains of CHMP2B[132](#ref-CR132 "Skibinski, G. et al. Mutations in the endosomal ESCRTIII-complex subunit CHMP2B in frontotemporal dementia. Nat. Genet. 37, 806–808 (2005)."),[133](#ref-CR133 "Cox, L. E. et al. Mutations in CHMP2B in lower motor neuron predominant amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS). PLoS ONE 5, e9872 (2010). References 132,133 identify mutations in CHMP2B as genetic causes of FTD/ALS."),[134](#ref-CR134 "van der Zee, J. et al. CHMP2B C-truncating mutations in frontotemporal lobar degeneration are associated with an aberrant endosomal phenotype in vitro. Hum. Mol. Genet. 17, 313–322 (2008)."),[135](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR135 "Urwin, H. et al. Disruption of endocytic trafficking in frontotemporal dementia with CHMP2B mutations. Hum. Mol. Genet 19, 2228–2238 (2010)."), suggestive of multiple mechanisms of disease.

Recent studies have shown that CHMP4B exhibits increased interaction with a mutant CHMP2B compared with overexpressed wild-type CHMP2B in HEK293 cells[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007)."). The mutant CHMP2B proteins do not dissociate from CHMP4B[132](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR132 "Skibinski, G. et al. Mutations in the endosomal ESCRTIII-complex subunit CHMP2B in frontotemporal dementia. Nat. Genet. 37, 806–808 (2005)."),[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007)."). Consistent with a role for CHMP2B mutations in abnormally stabilizing protein–protein interactions, CHMP2B mutants show increased association with spastin[137](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR137 "Gissawong, N. et al. Electrochemical detection of methyl parathion using calix[6]arene/bismuth ferrite/multiwall carbon nanotube-modified fluorine-doped tin oxide electrode. Mikrochim. Acta 189, 461 (2022).") compared with wild-type CHMP2B, which may in turn affect spastin function.

CHMP2B disease is characterized by SQSTM1 inclusions[138](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR138 "Holm, I. E., Englund, E., Mackenzie, I. R., Johannsen, P. & Isaacs, A. M. A reassessment of the neuropathology of frontotemporal dementia linked to chromosome 3. J. Neuropathol. Exp. Neurol. 66, 884–891 (2007).") and accumulation of large endosomes[135](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR135 "Urwin, H. et al. Disruption of endocytic trafficking in frontotemporal dementia with CHMP2B mutations. Hum. Mol. Genet 19, 2228–2238 (2010).") in postmortem patient tissues. These pathologic hallmarks have also been observed in mutant CHMP2B transgenic mice, along with autofluorescent lipid accumulations[139](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR139 "Jambroes, M., Lamkaddem, M., Stronks, K. & Essink-Bot, M. L. Enumerating the preventive youth health care workforce: Size, composition and regional variation in the Netherlands. Health Policy 119, 1557–1564 (2015)."),[140](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR140 "Ghazi-Noori, S. et al. Progressive neuronal inclusion formation and axonal degeneration in CHMP2B mutant transgenic mice. Brain 135, 819–832 (2012)."). These pathologic observations suggest that CHMP2B mutations disrupt the endolysosomal pathway. Supporting this idea, studies have demonstrated that overexpression of mutant CHMP2B impairs membrane scission, resulting in the accumulation of ubiquitinated proteins and malformed MVEs that are unable to fuse with lysosomes[134](#ref-CR134 "van der Zee, J. et al. CHMP2B C-truncating mutations in frontotemporal lobar degeneration are associated with an aberrant endosomal phenotype in vitro. Hum. Mol. Genet. 17, 313–322 (2008)."),[135](#ref-CR135 "Urwin, H. et al. Disruption of endocytic trafficking in frontotemporal dementia with CHMP2B mutations. Hum. Mol. Genet 19, 2228–2238 (2010)."),[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007)."). CHMP2B mutant proteins are often distributed in cytoplasmic puncta, as opposed to diffusely[132](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR132 "Skibinski, G. et al. Mutations in the endosomal ESCRTIII-complex subunit CHMP2B in frontotemporal dementia. Nat. Genet. 37, 806–808 (2005)."),[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007)."),[141](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR141 "West, R. J. H., Ugbode, C., Fort-Aznar, L. & Sweeney, S. T. Neuroprotective activity of ursodeoxycholic acid in CHMP2B(Intron5) models of frontotemporal dementia. Neurobiol. Dis. 144, 105047 (2020)."), and these colocalize with markers of early, late and recycling endosomes[132](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR132 "Skibinski, G. et al. Mutations in the endosomal ESCRTIII-complex subunit CHMP2B in frontotemporal dementia. Nat. Genet. 37, 806–808 (2005)."),[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007).") suggesting that mutant CHMP2B proteins directly affect endolysosomal trafficking and function. Moreover, a recent study demonstrated that expression of mutant CHMP2B led to the accumulation of phosphorylated TDP-43 in N2A cells[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007)."), thereby linking disruptions in endolysosomal protein turnover caused by CHMP2B mutation to impaired clearance of proteins.

At the synapse, overexpression of mutant CHMP2B has been linked to defects in synaptic vesicle cycling, dendritic retraction[136](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR136 "Lee, J. A., Beigneux, A., Ahmad, S. T., Young, S. G. & Gao, F. B. ESCRT-III dysfunction causes autophagosome accumulation and neurodegeneration. Curr. Biol. 17, 1561–1567 (2007).") and altered dendritic spine maturation[142](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR142 "Belly, A. et al. CHMP2B mutants linked to frontotemporal dementia impair maturation of dendritic spines. J. Cell Sci. 123, 2943–2954 (2010)."), highlighting additional disease mechanisms beyond the endolysosomal system. Although mutant CHMP2B proteins impair membrane sealing and repair[143](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR143 "Heffner, C. M., Starling, G. P., Isaacs, A. M. & Carlton, J. G. The ALS- and FTD-associated proteins annexin A11 and CHMP2B act sequentially in membrane repair. Preprint at bioRxiv
https://doi.org/10.1101/2024.11.19.624330
(2024)."), it is currently unknown how this may compromise cells in the central nervous system in neurodegeneration. Collectively, CHMP2B mutation studies suggest that similar to most proteins that are implicated in neurodegeneration, mutations in CHMP2B are likely to have consequences involving both loss and gain of function throughout the cell.

### Non-genetic contributions of ESCRT proteins

Recent studies have also begun to highlight a role for altered ESCRT protein localization and function in neurodegenerative disease pathogenesis even in the absence of ESCRT protein mutations. In sporadic ALS (sALS) and *C9orf72*-associated ALS/FTD patient cells and tissues, CHMP7 is abnormally relocalized from the cytoplasm to the nucleus in a manner that is dependent on the linker of nucleoskeleton and cytoskeleton (LINC) complex protein SUN1 and nuclear pore complex permeability barrier integrity[64](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR64 "Coyne, A. N. et al. Nuclear accumulation of CHMP7 initiates nuclear pore complex injury and subsequent TDP-43 dysfunction in sporadic and familial ALS. Sci. Transl. Med. 13, eabe1923 (2021)."),[144](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR144 "Baskerville, V., Rapuri, S., Mehlhop, E. & Coyne, A. N. SUN1 facilitates CHMP7 nuclear influx and injury cascades in sporadic amyotrophic lateral sclerosis. Brain 147, 109–121 (2024)."). Mechanistically, wild-type CHMP2B protein is necessary and sufficient to drive the overactivation of CHMP7 and ESCRT-III mediated nucleoporin turnover to elicit nuclear pore complex injury in an induced pluripotent stem cell-derived neuron model of sALS[145](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR145 "Keeley, O., Mendoza, E., Menon, D. & Coyne, A. N. CHMP2B promotes CHMP7 mediated nuclear pore complex injury in sporadic ALS. Acta Neuropathol. Commun. 12, 199 (2024)."), suggesting that CHMP2B may have a pivotal role in neurodegenerative disease pathogenesis. Critically, ESCRT-dependent pathological decreases in specific nucleoporins in the neuronal nuclear pore complex impairs nucleocytoplasmic transport and affects localization and function of the FTD-associated protein TDP-43[64](/articles/s41586-025-08950-y#ref-CR64 "Coyne, A.
